  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1808270071" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1808270071" />
    <a title="Erik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1639329-erik"><img alt="Erik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1440429204p2/1639329.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1808270071?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik" href="/user/show/1639329-erik">Erik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=scififantasy">scififantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1639329-erik?shelf=detailed-review">detailed-review</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1808270071" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11761362606747198566">Occasionally, I encounter this idea that a large – maybe even a majority – slice of science fiction media involves immature stories that focus heavily on pew pew lasers, explosions, and sex with attractive alien women (<a href='https://s-media-cache-ak0.pinimg.com/originals/a2/fe/88/a2fe88750e07c9ed088264bae40cba3b.jpg' rel='nofollow noopener'>Darth Talon</a>, <a href='http://s14.postimg.org/ky8ox4nu9/liararomance.png' rel='nofollow noopener'>Liara</a>, <a href='http://www.flavinscorner.com/GreenAlienGirl.jpg' rel='nofollow noopener'>Green Chick</a>). For example, Ted Chiang says that Star Wars forever made the genre synonymous with “adventure stories dressed up in lasers.” Or, as another example, NPR Book’s review of Ancillary Justice read, "If you don't know the Ancillary series by now, you p</span>
  <span id="freeText11761362606747198566" style="display:none">Occasionally, I encounter this idea that a large – maybe even a majority – slice of science fiction media involves immature stories that focus heavily on pew pew lasers, explosions, and sex with attractive alien women (<a href="https://s-media-cache-ak0.pinimg.com/originals/a2/fe/88/a2fe88750e07c9ed088264bae40cba3b.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">Darth Talon</a>, <a href="http://s14.postimg.org/ky8ox4nu9/liararomance.png" rel="nofollow noopener">Liara</a>, <a href="http://www.flavinscorner.com/GreenAlienGirl.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">Green Chick</a>). For example, Ted Chiang says that Star Wars forever made the genre synonymous with “adventure stories dressed up in lasers.” Or, as another example, NPR Book’s review of Ancillary Justice read, "If you don't know the Ancillary series by now, you probably should. Ann Leckie's sociopolitical space opera almost singlehandedly breathed new cool into the stereotype of spaceships trundling through far-off systems amid laser battles." In other words, there’s a perception that much of science fiction is aimed primarily at teenage boys, who are supposedly largely interested in action and sex.<br /><br />For the sake of focus, I’ll not dispute that claim in its entirety (though in my experience every part of it is more often untrue than true), but when I see this claim applied to science fiction LITERATURE, I become truly confused.<br /><br />Cause, um, what in the nine hells are these people talking about?? I’ve never read a science fiction novel or short story that was more focused on pew pew lasers or xeno-copulation than on sociology / science / technology / culture / ideas. Have you? Even YA sci-fi like Ender’s Game is far more interested in the psychology of a young genius or children in general than in space battles.<br /><br />More to the point, though, this entire dichotomy – adventure story OR science / depth / complexity / ideas – represents a fundamental misunderstanding of how science fiction operates. It uses the Trojan horse technique of hooking readers with a good story, so it can explore less immediately thrilling but ultimately more interesting complex science and societal concepts. Sci-fi is one of the few genres in which we get to have – and indeed must have – both the adventure AND the depth. To quote from Analog magazine’s submission page: “[Science fiction stories are those] in which some aspect of future science or technology is so integral to the plot that, if that aspect were removed, the story would collapse. Try to picture Mary Shelley's Frankenstein without the science and you'll see what I mean. No story!”<br /><br />Well, Endymion is a perfect example of how adventure and ideas combine to form science fiction. <br /><br />Now, yes, just to get it out of the way, I’ll add to that chorus of reviewers stating it’s not as good as Hyperion, but since Hyperion is one of the best science fiction books ever written, this is a bit like saying, “This ruby isn’t as good as this diamond.”<br /><br />Cause Endymion is a real gem. It has everything I want and expect from my science fiction. It’s got romance (albeit a weird Lolita-esque version that I’ll talk more about in my sequel review). It’s got fantastical settings, from jungle planets with mysterious ruins to ocean planets with massive sea monsters to high-gravity frozen planets populated by yetis &amp; ice wyrms. It’s got a flying carpet, prophecies and mysteries, a blue-skinned android, FTL travel, space marines, awesome battles, the return of the Shrike creature, dastardly villains, and more. In short, it’s unmistakably an ADVENTURE. Reading it is a bloody good time!<br /><br />But to dismiss Endymion on those grounds would be to ignore its thematic foundations of religion, time, death, belief, love, space, and AI.<br /><br />In Endymion, the relatively liberal values of the Hegemony have been replaced by more conservative anti-science, xenophobic ones – as one might expect given the catastrophic collapse of civilization at the end of Hyperion. The Catholic religion is back in a big way, and the primary means of their ascension is the adoption of the “sacrament” of the cruciform organism which grants immortality to all wearers. The pope (Father Lenar Hoyt from Hyperion) has died, revived, and been re-elected over and over. Meanwhile, the protagonist, one Raul Endymion, is recruited by our favorite poet Martin Silenus to meet a supposed savior, who has jumped forward some 250 years into the future via anti-entropic fields. And of course, lurking in the shadows and occasionally coming back into the light, is the AI TechnoCore. In fact, humanity’s interactions with the AI TechnoCore form the very basis of the plot, just as they did with Hyperion.<br /><br />This is especially interesting to me because I’ve lately come to believe that the development and ethics of AI is the single most important topic for humanity. If I made the claim that by 2100, humanity will either be extinct or immortal, you might scoff and think I’m being wildly optimistic. You might think I’m committing the same sort of error that Back to the Future made by predicting flying cars and hoverboards by 2015. Well you’d be wrong. The 2100 date is conservative. The median expert guess at when we will develop an ASI (Artificial Super Intelligence) is about 2060. After which, it won’t be long until we’re either immortal (likely in machine or software form) or extinct.<br /><br />Let me explain: if Moore’s Law holds true (<a href="https://www.extremetech.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/MooresLaw2.png" rel="nofollow noopener">and it has so far</a>), then by 2025 a cpu that matches the computational power of the human brain will be available for a relatively inexpensive price. If we’re conservative, we might say it’ll take 25 years after that (at which point our CPUs will be insanely powerful) until we’ve sufficiently understood the human brain in order to make our first Artificial General Intelligence (AGI).<br /><br />Once that happens, however, we enter a realm in which it’s no longer the HUMAN beings who will be improving the AI, but the AI itself. And as the AI gets smarter, the quicker it will be able to upgrade itself (which is also how human society works – this is called an accelerated rate of change and the recent US election shows how troublesome this is, since human institutions &amp; corresponding values / morals are MUCH slower to change than technology). Soon after that, the AGI will become an ASI, which will be to us, like we are to ants. It will possess God-like powers, wielded with unfathomable motivations. [If you're interested in AI topics, see my reviews for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1893522603" rel="nofollow noopener">Bostrom's Superintelligence</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1890802701" rel="nofollow noopener">Kurzweil's The Singularity is Near</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1814168857" rel="nofollow noopener">James Barrat's Our Final Invention</a>].<br /><br />Depending on how we introduce that ASI, it will either help us immensely or it’ll erase us from existence.<br /><br />While most agree that the latter option is definitely bad, the prospect of immortality wouldn’t necessarily be well-received either. Regardless, it’s important for us to begin introducing real AI (not the anthropomorphized Hollywood version) into the collective societal consciousness now. We need politicians to begin considering what their stances will be on AI issues. We need our children (and future scientists) to begin processing the idea of artificial intelligence, robot psychology, and the pros/cons of immortality. And of course, the dangers of ASI – which meet and exceed the dangers posed by global warming &amp; nuclear weapon proliferation – need to be a part of our conversation now because work that may/will lead to such a creature is happening NOW.<br /><br />And those are only future concerns! There’s plenty of AI-related issues to deal with right now. There exists a hedge fund that is entirely controlled by AI. Is that fair or wise? [See Nick Wolven’s short story: <a href="http://www.asimovs.com/assets/1/6/On_the_Night_of_the_RoboBulls_and_Zombie_Dancers.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Night of the Robo-bulls and Zombie Dancers</a>]. How about the current loss of manufacturing jobs to automation? Ignorance about that issue (that is, many people falsely believe manufacturing job loss is more a result of globalization than of mechanization) changed the course of an election, which in turn will likely change the American political and moral landscape for the next fifty years.<br /><br />So there’s a lot of great ideas in Endymion, which fills the spaces between its adventure set-pieces with moments of discussion and reflection. It strikes a wonderful balance between depth and plot, and I heartily enjoyed it. Perhaps more importantly, it made me EXCITED to explore both new sci-fi stories and non-fiction on AI and indeed excited about the possibilities of adventure and science in the future. What more can a sci-fi reader ask for?</span>
  <a data-text-id="11761362606747198566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_392855819" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/392855819" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/392855819?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/7991639-endymion">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">invincible spiky alien killing machines, Roman Catholic priest starship captains</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer392855819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7667560032188862078">Although it takes place centuries after <i>The Fall of Hyperion</i>, <i>Endymion</i> seems to pick up right after the end of the last book. This is the third book in Dan Simmons's "Hyperion Cantos." Since it's the first book of a second duology, you could start reading with this book, since the entire universe is pretty much introduced anew, but there are so many references to events that took place in the first two books, now history in this one, that you will probably feel like you're missing a lot.<br/><br/>At the e</span>
  <span id="freeText7667560032188862078" style="display:none">Although it takes place centuries after <i>The Fall of Hyperion</i>, <i>Endymion</i> seems to pick up right after the end of the last book. This is the third book in Dan Simmons's "Hyperion Cantos." Since it's the first book of a second duology, you could start reading with this book, since the entire universe is pretty much introduced anew, but there are so many references to events that took place in the first two books, now history in this one, that you will probably feel like you're missing a lot.<br /><br />At the end of <i>The Fall of Hyperion</i>, the Hegemony of Man was falling, due to the attack of the Ousters <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[who weren't really Ousters but constructs of the TechnoCore <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. As <i>Endymion</i> begins, the Hegemony is history, and now human space is ruled by the Pax; a resurgent Holy Roman Catholic Church empowered by the cruciform parasites we encountered in <i>Hyperion</i>, which allow anyone to recover from any injury and be resurrected from nearly any fatality. The Pax has figured out how to control them so that people who receive the cruciform are not turned into mindless idiots, which means that the Church now <em>literally</em> offers eternal life.<br /><br />The child of Brawne Lamia and the cybrid Johnny Keats emerges from the Time Tombs, and the Pax views her as a threat to all of mankind, for reasons that are not clear until the end. So they send starship captain Father-Captain de Soya to "fetch" her. Meanwhile, that irascible dirty old man Martin Silenus is still kicking around, and he recruits Raul Endymion, a native of the planet Hyperion who fell into a little trouble with the Pax, to go save her. As he tells Raul, he doesn't just want Raul to save his god-daughter from the Pax. He also wants Raul to destroy the Pax, find out what the superhuman artificial intelligences known as the TechnoCore have been up to these past few centuries, oh, and take down that enigmatic, unstoppable alien killing machine known as the Shrike. No problem.<br /><br /><i>Endymion</i> alternates between the POV of Raul Endymion and Father-Captain de Soya, adversaries but both of them ultimately good guys if not always serving good ends. There's plenty of interplanetary space opera drama and action, but for me it didn't really get good until the final few chapters when conspiracies begin to be unveiled, and of course, we finally got the kick-ass battle with the Shrike we've been waiting for.<br /><br />Like <i>Hyperion</i>, <i>Endymion</i> ends very much on a "To be continued" note. Either of the two duologies can be read independently, but definitely read the first book of each first (and if you like it you will certainly have to read the second).<br /><br />I recommend reading the first two books first because frankly, they are better. <i>Endymion</i> isn't bad, but it's a solid 3.5 stars - great epic space opera if you like epic space operas, but whereas Simmons dropped a whole lot of finely-crafted worldbuilding with star-spanning conspiracies and multiple existential alien threats in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121712780" rel="nofollow noopener">Hyperion</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151052136" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fall of Hyperion</a>, in this third book, there's not so much new as building on what he introduced before. If you are a dedicated consumer of space opera, this is above average for the genre, but falls short of greatness, and really I think the series could have ended with <i>Fall of Hyperion</i>. But I will go on to read the fourth and final volume.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7667560032188862078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2928395536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2928395536" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99853253-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602426860p2/99853253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2928395536?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/99853253-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/99853253-william?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2928395536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11892296007652009669">Endymion is a proper sequel to Dan Simmons's Hyperion books, in spite of what you may have read or heard. The timeline has advanced almost 300 years and we have some new characters, but the story of ascension is still in clear focus and well advanced.<br/><br/>While I do read a lot of reviews in finding "my next read", I try to avoid pre-conceived ideas. I may have failed in keeping an open mind in this case and been unfair to Endymion in the first 25% of the book. Thus, I started Endymion thinking it was</span>
  <span id="freeText11892296007652009669" style="display:none">Endymion is a proper sequel to Dan Simmons's Hyperion books, in spite of what you may have read or heard. The timeline has advanced almost 300 years and we have some new characters, but the story of ascension is still in clear focus and well advanced.<br /><br />While I do read a lot of reviews in finding "my next read", I try to avoid pre-conceived ideas. I may have failed in keeping an open mind in this case and been unfair to Endymion in the first 25% of the book. Thus, I started Endymion thinking it was not a sequel to The Fall of Hyperion, and that it was of lesser quality. Early on, Endymion did nothing to dissuade me from this notion; it was reading like a well-written science-fantasy adventure. Thinking back, this is also how Hyperion started; with the final Shrike pilgrims telling their stories as they set off on their journey. Granted, Endymion did seem lesser in scope, but this is not a measure of quality. As the book progressed, I remembered how the Hyperion books also spent a good portion of the book sowing the seeds of tension and lore, and Endymion similarly bloomed into a complex and engrossing story. So don't do what Mr. Windup Bird did and let negative reviews taint your expectations; Endymion fully deserves to be called a sequel, and a good one at that.<br /><br />We have some returning characters but the focus is on the new ones. We get to know Aenea, Raul, A Bettik, and Father Captain Fedrico de Soya as the story progresses. All were essential to the story, interesting, and acted consistently with how I thought they should act. There were fewer characters in Endymion (compared to Hyperion), but I found all of them very satisfying, and they helped keep the story interesting. Inter-character relationships were complex, believable, and endearing, growing into a strong party dynamic that made me care about each of them even more.<br /><br />Endymion is set on the Tethys; a great river traversing hundreds of web worlds via farcaster. As such the story takes place on a number of diverse worlds in a state of decline or ruin after "The Fall" at the end of The Fall of Hyperion. Such a fantastic job is done with the setting in Endymion, in particular, the frozen waste of Sol Draconi Septem. I had a lot of fun imagining the setting and characters in this part of the book, though all of the worlds our adventurers visited were imaginative and thought-provoking. As the raft floated down the Tethys, I couldn't help but be reminded of another favorite book, Riverworld by Philip Jose Farmer. I loved the "many different worlds on a river" idea then, and even more now.<br /><br />The story pulled me in with a well-thought-out rescue and chase adventure, then later revealed a more complex intrigue connecting the first two books. I was never bored or tempted to put the book down and very happy with the lead-in to The Rise of Endymion, which I will be starting immediately after finishing this review.<br /><br />I apologize for starting your book with low expectations Dan Simmons, it won't happen again.<br /><br />Pleasantly surprised! 5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="11892296007652009669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1687401960" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1687401960" />
    <a title="Sumant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21982745-sumant"><img alt="Sumant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373196404p2/21982745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1687401960?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sumant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sumant" href="/user/show/21982745-sumant">Sumant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21982745-sumant?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21982745-sumant?shelf=hyperion-cantos">hyperion-cantos</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1687401960" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9670052815736279114">I knew that <strong>entropy</strong> existed in the universe, but <strong>Endymion</strong> proves that it applies to great book series too. The only word which I can think after finishing this book is <strong>disappointment</strong>. The series just took nose dive for me into <strong>oblivion</strong> in this book. I just can't find the words to express my disappointment regarding this book, and this is partly due to the fact that I really liked the first two books in <strong>Hyperion</strong> series. <br/><br/>Some of the weak points of the book are<br/><br/>1.<em>The story seems to be going no where</em></span>
  <span id="freeText9670052815736279114" style="display:none">I knew that <strong>entropy</strong> existed in the universe, but <strong>Endymion</strong> proves that it applies to great book series too. The only word which I can think after finishing this book is <strong>disappointment</strong>. The series just took nose dive for me into <strong>oblivion</strong> in this book. I just can't find the words to express my disappointment regarding this book, and this is partly due to the fact that I really liked the first two books in <strong>Hyperion</strong> series. <br /><br />Some of the weak points of the book are<br /><br />1.<em>The story seems to be going no where.</em><br />2.<em>Flat characters.</em><br />3.<em>Deus ex machine syndrome.</em><br /><br />Let me elaborate on the above points<br /><br />1.<strong>The story seems to be going no where.</strong><br /><br />The book definitely starts strongly when we are introduced to the lead character in the series <em>Raul Endymion</em>, we are also given his background story and shown the circumstances due to which he has ended where is now.<br /><br /><em>Simmons</em> uses the solid world initially and some of the <em>pilgrim</em> characters from the previous books also make a comeback, but once this is done the <em>chase</em> starts then actually the problem with the book starts.<br /><br />This book should have been aptly titled <em>Hitchhiker's guide to hegemony</em> because our protagonist seem to travel through travel different planets for the heck of it, without giving us the reader any motive for this travel. <br /><br />The travelogues consists basically of huge descriptions regarding of atmosphere of the planet along with its flora and fauna and also the civilization of that planet. Although it starts interestingly but this pattern keeps on repeating itself until finally <em>Simmons</em> realizes that the book should end where in he gives us some meaning for this travel and raps up the book. <br /><br />2.<strong>Flat characters.</strong><br /><br />The previous books were interesting due to the fact that, they had some amazing characters in them and each of their story explained to us the world of <em>hegemony</em>. We did not need huge info dumps regarding anything in this world as it flowed fluidly with the story.<br /><br />The less said the better regarding the characters in this book, because I was not able to connect with any of the characters in this book, I cared for the previous characters and wanted to know what happened to each of them in previous books, but in <strong>Endymion</strong> I just did not give a damn as to what was happening to the characters.<br /><br />3.<strong>Deus ex machine syndrome.</strong><br /><br />It was ok to have a <em>Deus ex machine aka Shrike</em> in the previous books, but in <strong>Endymion</strong> the author just solves any problem with this strategy. Also having one <em>Deus ex machine</em> is understandable, but when you have three of them throughout the story then for me something is terribly wrong with the story.<br /><br />Although I did not like this book a bit I am still going to give it 2 stars and these are due to the fact that I really liked the first two books in this series, and hope that the last book is better than this one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9670052815736279114" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer580267808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16091828365441884201"><b>Endymion: A New Phase of the Hyperion Story</b><br/>Endymion takes place roughly three centuries after the events of Hyperion and Fall of Hyperion. It features a largely new cast of characters, and the climatic events of the previous book are so far-reaching in their impact (the closing of the far-casters, end of the hegemony, disappearance of the Shrike, rise of the Pax thanks to the cruciform, etc) that Simmons has created a very new and different galactic milieu in which to frame his equally complex a</span>
  <span id="freeText16091828365441884201" style="display:none"><b>Endymion: A New Phase of the Hyperion Story</b><br />Endymion takes place roughly three centuries after the events of Hyperion and Fall of Hyperion. It features a largely new cast of characters, and the climatic events of the previous book are so far-reaching in their impact (the closing of the far-casters, end of the hegemony, disappearance of the Shrike, rise of the Pax thanks to the cruciform, etc) that Simmons has created a very new and different galactic milieu in which to frame his equally complex and elaborate space opera epic. <br /><br />While Endymion may lack some of the mythic overtones of the first duology, it makes up for that with a fully-developed narrative that carefully extrapolates the repercussions of the previous books and continues the underlying struggle/mystery of the Core and AIs and the humans and Ousters caught between it all. Dan Simmons is not afraid to reshape his own elaborate universe and yet treat it as a natural extension of the earlier story. Just like the first two books, Endymion is only one half of the story, so you must be prepared to commit to reading both books to get the full vision as Dan Simmons intended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16091828365441884201" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8719229422077081116">Wow-I have a new favorite!<br/><br/>I truly loved this book all the way through. Have you ever read a book where right away you just knew you were going to love it, and tried to read it real slow so you could savor it? that was me with Endymion....<br/><br/>Here we follow the story of Aenea, the child of Hyperion characters John Keats and Brawnia...who we learn is destined to be a major prophet.<br/><br/>This is the story of how she managed to be 3 centuries into the future after the events of Fall of Hyperion, and her pilgr</span>
  <span id="freeText8719229422077081116" style="display:none">Wow-I have a new favorite!<br /><br />I truly loved this book all the way through. Have you ever read a book where right away you just knew you were going to love it, and tried to read it real slow so you could savor it? that was me with Endymion....<br /><br />Here we follow the story of Aenea, the child of Hyperion characters John Keats and Brawnia...who we learn is destined to be a major prophet.<br /><br />This is the story of how she managed to be 3 centuries into the future after the events of Fall of Hyperion, and her pilgrimage to Old Earth, as told by Raul Endymion, who will someday be her true love.<br /><br />Although this is all a precursor to whatever destiny she will fulfill (I assume that is the story in Endymion Rising), this story bring new clues to some of the events and happenings of the first two Hyperion books, creates some beautiful new characters, and resolves the fates of some of the old ones.<br /><br />Highly recommended! </span>

Raul becomes a reluctant hero, often plunging into things (sometimes literally) because he has been told that is his role. Would he have done all these things without that subtle coercion? Maybe.<br/><br/>I'm still kind of surprised at how many books set in the far future still include influential religions from Earth, especially the Catholic Church.   <span id="freeText14376132508033802649" style="display:none">Part of my 2020 Social Distancing Read-a-thon<br /><br />3.5 stars<br /><br />An interesting exploration of the nature of heroism. <div id="review_73344158" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73344158" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73344158?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=artificial-intelligence">artificial-intelligence</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=posthuman">posthuman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73344158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13646884924905577677">Centuries after the events of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73344154' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <em>The Fall of Hyperion</em>
</a>, and three and a half years after I read that book, <em>Endymion</em> takes place and I read it. I had actually forgotten that there was a book between this one and <em>Hyperion</em>; I described this as the second book in a series when friends asked me what I was reading. Oops! And it has been so long since I read the first two that my memories of the series were distant and vague.<br/><br/>That proved not as much of a barrier as I worried it would be. I’m still tryin</span>
  <span id="freeText13646884924905577677" style="display:none">Centuries after the events of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73344154" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <em>The Fall of Hyperion</em>
</a>, and three and a half years after I read that book, <em>Endymion</em> takes place and I read it. I had actually forgotten that there was a book between this one and <em>Hyperion</em>; I described this as the second book in a series when friends asked me what I was reading. Oops! And it has been so long since I read the first two that my memories of the series were distant and vague.<br><br>That proved not as much of a barrier as I worried it would be. I’m still trying to figure out why I am so ambivalent towards Dan Simmons’ other work but loving the <em>Hyperion Cantos</em>. It isn’t the classical allusions—as much as I love the classics, that doesn’t work so much for me. But the books in this series are just so well constructed, characterized, and compelling in their depth and scope, that I’m happy to claim that this series represents some of the finest far-future space opera of the nineties.<br><br>It took me a little while to get a feel for <em>Endymion</em>. I wasn’t enjoying the introduction to Raul, his meeting with Martin Silenus, etc. Once Simmons introduces Father Captain de Soya and the hunt for Aenea, however, things pick up considerably. The way in which he cuts between the two perspectives of hunter and hunted works quite well. Ideally when an author does this, they manage to make you constantly yearn for both perspectives: just as it switches from Raul to de Soya we’re supposed to wonder how the fugitives will get out of their latest cliffhanger. I admit to some preference for de Soya’s story—but that’s mostly because I was so intrigued by the internal affairs of the Pax.<br><br>When we last saw the Pax, it was a growing political movement on Pacem—but now it has taken the place of the Hegemony in the former Web worlds. Thanks to the collapse of the fatline and data spheres, the Pax has an information monopoly that allows them to manipulate public perception (e.g., of things like the Ousters). Yet Simmons hints that, despite the piety implicit in Pax life, there are more sinister elements in the upper echelons of the Church. In Father Captain de Soya he creates a great antihero: sincere in his belief and devotion to God and the Church, de Soya nevertheless has enough independent thought to begin questioning when the facts stop adding up. He is an antagonist in the sense that he is working against our protagonists’ ends—but he is not a bad man or a villain by any means.<br><br>I didn’t really warm up to Raul. He’s not a bad character, in that he isn’t too whiny. He’s just not the type of main character I want to identify with too much … I never got any grasp on his personality beyond a sense of competence and occasional references to his grandmother. I found that I best enjoyed the chapters with him, Bettik, and Aenea touring the River Tethys if I ignored the overall plot and just focused on the dangers they faced on each planet.<br><br>These subplots turn <em>Endymion</em> from what could be a weak-but-sprawling space opera into a fluid-but-lengthy adventure story. The three fugitives face a new challenge on every world, always escaping by the skin of their teeth. Simmons finds the right balance between no exposition and too much as he reveals just enough to keep us guessing about the identities of those who are helping Aenea and their relationship to the Pax, which is so concerned with apprehending her. There are plenty of allusions to the events of the past two books—and I’d recommend reading them before reading this one—but by and large, <em>Endymion</em> is much more about Aenea’s personal development than wider galactic affairs.<br><br>She keeps referring to being guided towards an architect who can teach her. Simmons hints that Aenea will be a messiah, someone special with “powers.” Fortunately, he avoids the temptation of turning her into a creepy child who manifests those powers early. Aside from a psychic episode here or there, she has to rely on her own determination and resolve—plus the help from Raul and Bettik—to survive. I loved the moment where she pointed out that, from the moment she stepped from the Time Tombs, it has all been one “very long day” for her.<br><br><em>Endymion</em> is long. But I actually like that about it. My weariness was sympathetic with the weariness the fugitives felt after their long journey, and with the weariness of de Soya and his minions for their constant deaths and resurrections. Simmons underscores how gallivanting through the galaxy is not a game for the merely human: space travel of any kind places demands on us that exceed what our bodies and minds evolved to handle. Though the TechnoCore’s role in this book is greatly reduced, Simmons reminds us that the existence of AI is a thorny existential issue for humanity.<br><br>In some ways, this book feels like filler between the conflicts begun in <em>The Fall of Hyperion</em> and what will hopefully be the resolution in <em>The Rise of Endymion</em>. I still enjoyed it, though, and heartily recommend it to those who read the first two books.<br><br><strong>My reviews of the <em>Hyperion Cantos</em>:</strong><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73344154" rel="nofollow noopener">← <em>The Fall of Hyperion</em></a> | <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73344162" rel="nofollow noopener"><em>The Rise of Endymion</em> →</a><br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/4.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1388723110i/7909581.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13646884924905577677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_955428651" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/955428651" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24844129-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393538805p2/24844129.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/955428651?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/24844129-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24844129-joe?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer955428651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16677137266738895312">Endymion by Dan Simmons is a Science Fiction novel on a grand space opera level with a very personal and detailed feel to the characters. It is the third in the Hyperion Cantos series.<br/><br/>The story starts off 274 years after the events of the second book, The Fall Of Hyperion. Raul Endymion, a shepherd and hunting guide living on Hyperion. He gets convicted of a murder and sentenced to death. After his execution things start to become far more complicated and difficult for him. He meets a familiar f</span>
  <span id="freeText16677137266738895312" style="display:none">Endymion by Dan Simmons is a Science Fiction novel on a grand space opera level with a very personal and detailed feel to the characters. It is the third in the Hyperion Cantos series.<br /><br />The story starts off 274 years after the events of the second book, The Fall Of Hyperion. Raul Endymion, a shepherd and hunting guide living on Hyperion. He gets convicted of a murder and sentenced to death. After his execution things start to become far more complicated and difficult for him. He meets a familiar face from the past and is told he must go to the Time Tombs, rescue a young girl called Aenea from church controlled military forces when she leaves the Shrike Temple, and then escort her so her message can be heard at the right time and place. They are not allowed to merely stroll away without complications however. The Pax, the restored Roman church and religion, sends Father Captain De Soya after them to bring Aenea to Church control. <br /><br />There is a small selection of continuous characters throughout the book; Raul, Aenea, the android A. Bettik, and Father Captain De Soya. The characters are all described extremely well, both physically and mentally. You quickly learn who they are and what drives them and motivates them to the acts they perform. As with the previous two books you get a true sense of personality and empathy with almost all major and most minor characters.<br /><br />The world building is once again very impressive. After the events of the last book and the intervening 274 years there are many places and societies that have changed. The changes to familiar places feel very believable and the sense of continuity is maintained. Simmons portrays the idea that people, societies and entire worlds evolve and continue no matter what anyone or anything can do. I personally loved this about the book and felt involved in the worlds and places their little group visits.<br /><br />In the first book, Hyperion, the pilgrims were on a gradual journey where you learn about them and the world they live in. Endymion has a similar feel to it; with the characters being chased across multiple worlds and environments learning what they can about each other and what they may need to do to accomplish their primary goals. <br /><br /><br />In Summary: An amazing SF novel with good characters and impressive world that manages to stay faithful to the world and feel of the first two books. It may not be quite as good as Hyperion but I still thought it was excellent and gave it 5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16677137266738895312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2224205445" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="SAM" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57392386-sam"><img alt="SAM" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556638019p2/57392386.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2224205445?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="SAM" class="user" itemprop="url" name="SAM" href="/user/show/57392386-sam">SAM</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57392386-sam?shelf=2018">2018</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2224205445" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17061569780491310917">I wasn't expecting this to be as good as Hyperion. A writer pens a classic and very rarely do they equal its awesomeness and originality. Endymion started great. It delivered all of the science fiction intrigue i've come to expect from Dan Simmons. The plot was nicely linked to the other two books and the whole idea of 'what message will she deliver' was interesting. But the more i read the less i liked. It was around page 400 i started to become bored. There's a couple of chapters of planet des</span>
  <span id="freeText17061569780491310917" style="display:none">I wasn't expecting this to be as good as Hyperion. A writer pens a classic and very rarely do they equal its awesomeness and originality. Endymion started great. It delivered all of the science fiction intrigue i've come to expect from Dan Simmons. The plot was nicely linked to the other two books and the whole idea of 'what message will she deliver' was interesting. But the more i read the less i liked. It was around page 400 i started to become bored. There's a couple of chapters of planet descriptions, journeying through tunnels and interacting with a new race of people which sucked the life out of my desire to give a damn. The 'message' the girl is carrying isn't explained until toward the end, which is too late because by then i was ready for the book to end. The main character, Raul, isn't the easiest person to like as he starts to become whiney and is overshadowed by Aenea. I would have liked more from Father De Soya as his mission is more interesting than Raul's. <br /><br />A little disappointed but i will read Rise of Endymion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17061569780491310917" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1131307471" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1131307471" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1131307471?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1131307471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer31470507975947796">You ever get those novels that just fucking provide endless consternation and cognitive dissonance when you're trying to slot them into one of the somewhat restrictive Goodreads rankings? <i>Endymion</i> is the biggest bastard of all of these kinds of books I occasionally experience. I honestly think that two-to-four rankings are the least fun to give out because when you're actually considering the book in the process of reading it and writing it up you have to work with it cerebrally and not on a vis</span>
  <span id="freeText31470507975947796" style="display:none">You ever get those novels that just fucking provide endless consternation and cognitive dissonance when you're trying to slot them into one of the somewhat restrictive Goodreads rankings? <i>Endymion</i> is the biggest bastard of all of these kinds of books I occasionally experience. I honestly think that two-to-four rankings are the least fun to give out because when you're actually considering the book in the process of reading it and writing it up you have to work with it cerebrally and not on a visceral level. It's hard to be reasonable and measured while simultaneously looking at a work at length and not just saying shit like "it had good parts...but it also had bad parts" over and over again. Whereas with the one and five star books you can just go in with emotional guns fucking blazing and just be easily inspired to new levels of moon-eyed adulation or derisive, incendiary rhetoric and all that good stuff that we humans love.<br /><br />See what I mean? I barely addressed the fucking book in my first paragraph! I don't want to talk about it. It's hard to say this without sounding haughty or pretentious but the first two <i>Hyperion</i> novels kind of transcended the usual sci-fi work. Sure, they had laser guns and brain-melting time travel and all that shit, but they also boasted the author's ability to examine huge themes like the importance of literature and poetry and humanity's fate in the far future WHILE BEING COMPULSORILY READABLE! <i>Endymion</i> is basically...an adventure story set in the same universe as the previous books but taking place about three hundred years later. It sounds like I'm slighting the book right off the bat and maybe I am a tiny bit, but it just didn't have quite the same feeling and atmosphere of constant, simultaneous wonder and terror that the last two invoked during my experiences with them. I think this is mostly due to the fact that the story is basically a really long, entertaining chase scene. I mean...this is still Dan Simmons writing these so I'll take a long space chase in the <i>Hyperion</i> universe over most other books out there.<br /><br />Just setting up the world and characters of the book to someone who didn't read the first two novels would be spoiling a ton of shit for them, so I'll avoid them in this review. I will say that I didn't like the characters nearly as much as I did the ones in the previous books. To be honest, I probably liked de Soya and M. Bettik the most, and the latter is a goddamn blue android--not the most human character to relate to, but that might be my problem and not Simmons'. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I can't refrain from mentioning my still-favorite character from the books, Martin Silenus. I was so happy when he showed up, even if he did look like a terrifying life-support mummy or whatever. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Raul was an interesting and convincing narrative voice and I was on his side but he was not the most vivid or well-developed character, which was weird as we were inside his fucking brain for half the book. Aenea was...well, I like to think that characters like these suffer from what I call St. Alia Syndrome, after Alia Atrides from <i>Dune</i>. It's kind of like the Scary Child trope from horror movies mixed with a kind of prescience, power or knowledge you would never, ever give to a child. Basically I liked Aenea but had to hold her at emotional arm's length because she, y'know...<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[is the messianic daughter of one of the chosen human pilgrims and a goddamn cybrid with cryptic ties to the TechnoCore and the Void Which Binds. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I'm done with my complaints now, I think. Main disappointments out of the way, this is still a great book and has some of the coolest space opera action you can find. The universe Simmons has built is still endlessly fascinating just to be in and you get to revisit several of the epic planets from the previous novels. The Shrike is still the best/worst thing ever, so that hasn't changed either. It was also novel to look at the Shrike as <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[kind of on the side of the good guys as opposed to its usual kill-on-sight self <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. All of the religious stuff in this one is also extremely interesting and well-done. With Simmons, when he writes about potential futures for humanity I don't just enjoy it in a "that's a cool idea" way, I examine that shit as if it has a real possibility or plausibility. I mean, who can really say that the Catholic Church has absolutely no chance of a long-lasting future? Considering that the Church considers St. Peter to be the first Pope and dude died in like fucking 60 BC and the title still exists and holds religious power post-Reformation, post-Enlightenment, post-"God is dead", post-sex-abuse-scandal why wouldn't there be a fucking Space Pope?<br /><br />Against most other books I've read, this is a masterpiece of fiction. Against the previous two novels in the series, this was a disappointment. I just can't be happy with any kind of letup on the throttle because I was perfectly happy where I was at with <i>Hyperion</i> and <i>Fall of Hyperion</i>. I've tasted the glory of Simmons' best and I can't be fully satisfied with anything less at this point because I know it's fucking out there, man, I've been there and I've seen it with my own two eyes. Anyone who knows what an avid fanboy I've been of this guy's work in the past knows that giving this less than five stars was a triumph of fucking will and something equatable to realizing you love one of your children just a little bit less than the others.* I just have to stay objective so as not to anger the Gods of amateur literary criticism, for they are merciless and ever-watchful.<br /><br />*Okay, that's obviously not true. But it was still painful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="31470507975947796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kira" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/264033-kira"><img alt="Kira" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441939671p2/264033.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72562276?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kira" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kira" href="/user/show/264033-kira">Kira</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/264033-kira?shelf=read_in_2012">read_in_2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72562276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2735897785750126622">Definitely the weakest of the four, although it might not be strictly fair to lump the tetralogy together that way -- Hyperion and Fall of Hyperion are basically one book stretched over two, even though the writing style changes. Endymion is in some ways a sequel, if one is looking strictly for chronological continuance of the Universe these characters inhabit. It's less so when one considers how brilliant and creative the first two books were, chocked with plot and heavy on characterization, wh</span>
  <span id="freeText2735897785750126622" style="display:none">Definitely the weakest of the four, although it might not be strictly fair to lump the tetralogy together that way -- Hyperion and Fall of Hyperion are basically one book stretched over two, even though the writing style changes. Endymion is in some ways a sequel, if one is looking strictly for chronological continuance of the Universe these characters inhabit. It's less so when one considers how brilliant and creative the first two books were, chocked with plot and heavy on characterization, while Endymion reads more like the action-adventure prequel to a much cooler, satisfying Rise of Endymion. I *like* Aenea and Raul, and I even appreciate how Simmons crafts their personalities, but everything else is just sort of ...blah. The explanations for a lot of "wait a minute...what?" moments in this novel don't come until the end of Rise, and even though that was also the case with the two Hyperion books, this wait seems much more protracted.<br /><br />I consider it the Hump Book of the series, in a lot of ways. (No, not in that way. They don't get jiggy until Rise!) Once you get over it, it's relatively smooth sailing to the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2735897785750126622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>

<li class='cover' id='bookCover_77711'>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('63y3nolIqlKCEdR8lwWsdFa6OxXKjWq8g5HfPw10OVeZHJsS+TXnQaz+GOGQ6+ejpkUsBTvHDgdYudp8wAWtkQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7se78C4R+WZLH9BUnorLZrWgTgKV//ByGc/zhJj9J1Wcp5d8Xmy0dWXwHMmZZICxRV9ZEmS1lMnC5/bHVYyzkw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons">About Dan Simmons</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1427999015p3/2687.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons">Dan Simmons
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer40896250325590768"><b>Dan Simmons</b> grew up in various cities and small towns in the Midwest, including Brimfield, Illinois, which was the source of his fictional "Elm Haven" in 1991's SUMMER OF NIGHT and 2002's A WINTER HAUNTING. Dan received a B.A. in English from Wabash College in 1970, winning a national Phi Beta Kappa Award during his senior year for excellence in fiction, journalism and art. <br/><br/>Dan received his Master</span>
  <span id="freeText40896250325590768" style="display:none"><b>Dan Simmons</b> grew up in various cities and small towns in the Midwest, including Brimfield, Illinois, which was the source of his fictional "Elm Haven" in 1991's SUMMER OF NIGHT and 2002's A WINTER HAUNTING. Dan received a B.A. in English from Wabash College in 1970, winning a national Phi Beta Kappa Award during his senior year for excellence in fiction, journalism and art. <br /><br />Dan received his Masters in Education from Washington University in St. Louis in 1971. He then worked in elementary education for 18 years—2 years in Missouri, 2 years in Buffalo, New York—one year as a specially trained BOCES "resource teacher" and another as a sixth-grade teacher—and 14 years in Colorado.<br /> <br /> ABOUT DAN<br /> Biographic Sketch <br /> <br />His last four years in teaching were spent creating, coordinating, and teaching in APEX, an extensive gifted/talented program serving 19 elementary schools and some 15,000 potential students. During his years of teaching, he won awards from the Colorado Education Association and was a finalist for the Colorado Teacher of the Year. He also worked as a national language-arts consultant, sharing his own "Writing Well" curriculum which he had created for his own classroom. Eleven and twelve-year-old students in Simmons' regular 6th-grade class averaged junior-year in high school writing ability according to annual standardized and holistic writing assessments. Whenever someone says "writing can't be taught," Dan begs to differ and has the track record to prove it. Since becoming a full-time writer, Dan likes to visit college writing classes, has taught in New Hampshire's Odyssey writing program for adults, and is considering hosting his own Windwalker Writers' Workshop.<br /><br />Dan's first published story appeared on Feb. 15, 1982, the day his daughter, Jane Kathryn, was born. He's always attributed that coincidence to "helping in keeping things in perspective when it comes to the relative importance of writing and life."<br /><br />Dan has been a full-time writer since 1987 and lives along the Front Range of Colorado—in the same town where he taught for 14 years—with his wife, Karen, his daughter, Jane, (when she's home from Hamilton College) and their Pembroke Welsh Corgi, Fergie. He does much of his writing at Windwalker—their mountain property and cabin at 8,400 feet of altitude at the base of the Continental Divide, just south of Rocky Mountain National Park. An 8-ft.-tall sculpture of the Shrike—a thorned and frightening character from the four Hyperion/Endymion novels—was sculpted by an ex-student and friend, Clee Richeson, and the sculpture now stands guard near the isolated cabin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="40896250325590768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
